Why Kalam is a Communal Obligation
Ghazali classifies kalam as fard kifaya and defends it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali classifies the study of kalam as a communal obligation (fard kifaya) rather than an individual one (fard 'ayn), and the reasoning behind this classification.

Core Idea
Kalam is a communal shield against persistent doubts, not a personal requirement for every believer.

Having established the importance of firm affirmation in the second preliminary, Ghazali now addresses a crucial practical question: who must actually master the science of kalam? In this passage, he argues that deep expertise in kalam is not required of every individual, but the community as a whole must ensure that some members are qualified to defend the faith. He anticipates an objection and provides a compelling analogy to drive his point home.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the opening line where Ghazali states that kalam is a communal obligation (fard kifaya), not an individual obligation (fard 'ayn). Notice how he immediately refers back to a previous proof.
This is a legal classification, not a mere opinion.
2
Identify the objection
Look for the phrase 'fa-in qulta' (if you say). Ghazali presents an imagined objection: if most sects don't benefit, why is it obligatory at all?
This objection challenges the very need for kalam.
3
Understand the reply
Ghazali answers by pointing out that doubt is possible, even if rare, and that innovators can arise at any time. The response reveals the underlying reason for the communal obligation.
The key is the potential for harm, not its frequency.
4
Examine the analogy
The final sentence compares the necessity of a kalam scholar to that of a physician and a jurist. Reflect on why this analogy strengthens his argument.
The analogy shows that communal obligation is recognized in other fields too.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التمهيد الثالث في بيان أن الاشتغال بهذا العالم من فروض الكفايات اعلم : أنَّ التبحرَ في هذا العلم والاشتغال بمجامعه ليس من فروض الأعيان ، بل هو من فروض الكفايات الثاني فأما أنَّه ليس من فروض الأعيان.. فقد اتضح لك برهانه في التمهيد (١) ؛ إذ تبيَّنَ أنَّه ليس يجب على كافة الخلق إلا التصديق الجازم وتطهير القلب عن الريب والشك في الإيمان ، وإنَّما تصيرُ إزالة الشك فرضَ عين في حق من اعتراه الشكُ تحريجة : فلم هو من فإن قلت : فلِمَ صار من فروض الكفايات ، وقد ذكرت أن أكثر الفرق فروض الكفايات ؟ يضرُّهم ذلك ولا ينفعهم ؟ فاعلم : أنَّه قد سبق أنَّ إزالة الشكوك في أصول العقائد واجبةٌ ، واعتوار الشك غير مستحيل وإن كان لا يقع إلا في الأقل ، ثم الدعوة إلى الحق بالبرهان لمن هو مصر على الباطل ، ومحتمل بذكائه لفهم البراهين .. مهم تثوير البدع والشكوك في الدين، ثم لا يبعد أن يثور مبتدع ويتصدى لإغواء أهل الحق بإفاضة محتمل في كل زمان الشبهة فيهم ، فلا بد ممَّنْ يقاوم شبهته بالكشف ، ويعارض إغواءه بالتقبيح ولا يمكن ذلك إلا بالعلم ، ولا تنفكُ البلاد عن أمثال هذه الوقائع ، فوجب أن يكون في كل قطر من الأقطار وصقع من الأصقاع قائم بالحقِّ ، مشتغل بهذا العلم ، يقاوم دعاة المبتدعة ، ويستميلُ المائلين عن الحقِّ ، ويصفي قلوب أهل السنة عن عوارض الشبهة ، فلو خلا عنه القطر .. حَرِجَ به أهلُ القطر كافة ؛ كما لو خلا عن الطبيب والفقيه
The Third Preliminary: Concerning the clarification that engagement in this world [i.e., this science] is from the communal obligations. Know that deep immersion in this science and engagement with its totalities is not from the individual obligations, but rather from the communal obligations. As for its not being from the individual obligations, its proof has become clear to you in the Second Preliminary, since it was clarified that nothing is obligatory upon all creatures except firm affirmation and purifying the heart from doubt and uncertainty in faith, and the removal of doubt only becomes an individual obligation for one whom doubt befalls. [Here there is a difficulty?] So if you say: Why has it become from the communal obligations, while you have mentioned that most sects [do not benefit from it]? Does it harm them and not benefit them? Then know: It has preceded that the removal of doubts in the principles of beliefs is obligatory, and the occurrence of doubt is not impossible, even if it only occurs in a minority. Then the call to truth by proof for one who is persistent upon falsehood and capable through his intelligence of understanding proofs is important for stirring up innovations and doubts in religion. Then it is not unlikely that an innovator arises and sets out to seduce the people of truth by pouring forth doubts upon them in every time. So there must be someone who resists his doubt by uncovering [the truth] and opposes his seduction by exposing its ugliness. And that is not possible except through knowledge. And the lands are never free from such occurrences. So it is necessary that in every region and district there be one who stands for truth, engaged in this science, who resists the callers of the innovators, and wins over those who deviate from the truth, and purifies the hearts of the Ahl al-Sunna from the accidents of doubt. So if a region is devoid of such a person, all the people of that region become sinful, just as if it is devoid of a physician and a jurist.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal classification and argument. Ghazali begins by stating the third preliminary: engagement in this science (i.e., ‘ilm al-kalam) is a communal obligation (fard kifaya), not an individual obligation (fard ‘ayn). He refers back to the second preliminary for the proof that only firm affirmation (tasdiq jazim) and removing doubt when it arises are individually obligatory. Then he presents an anticipated objection (fa-in qulta): 'Why is it a communal obligation if most sects do not benefit from it and it does not harm them?' Ghazali answers by first reiterating that removing doubts in foundational beliefs is obligatory, and doubt is possible even if rare. Then he argues that the call to truth with rational proofs (burhan) is important to counteract those who persistently cling to falsehood and are intellectually capable. Because innovators may arise at any time and spread doubts, there must be someone in every region who is skilled in kalam to resist them, to win back those who have deviated, and to purify the hearts of Ahl al-Sunna from doubts. If a region lacks such a person, all its inhabitants become sinful, similar to a region without a physician or a jurist. Thus, the obligation is communal: if enough qualified persons exist, the obligation is fulfilled; if none exist, the entire community is blameworthy.

Key Terms
فروض الكفايات
فروض الأعيان
إزالة الشكوك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that kalam is an optional luxury or only for scholars, while Ghazali shows it is a communal obligation necessary for the defense of the faith.
Correction: Kalam is not optional; the community is sinful if no one is proficient in it, because doubts and innovators will always arise.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is ‘ilm al-kalam a communal obligation (fard kifaya) rather than an individual obligation?
A) Because all Muslims must study it deeply.
B) Because the removal of doubt is only required for scholars.
C) Because innovators and doubts can arise at any time, requiring someone in each region to counter them.
D) Because it is more important than medicine and jurisprudence.

Key Takeaway
The study of kalam is a communal obligation because doubts and innovators never cease, and the entire community shares the sin if no one is able to counter them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you lived in a region with no one skilled in kalam, would you feel personally accountable for the spread of doubts? What steps could you take to fulfill that communal obligation even as a non-specialist?
?
Do you ever dismiss theological discussions as irrelevant to your daily life, forgetting that your own faith could be shaken by doubts? How will you strengthen your foundation so that you are not caught off guard?
?
The analogy with the physician suggests that ignoring the health of the community's beliefs is as dangerous as ignoring physical health. In your current priorities, do you give the defense of the faith the same urgency as you give to practical needs? What change will you make today?

Ilm al-Kalam as a Communal Obligation
Ghazali states that engaging in kalam is a communal obligation and proves it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali classifies the study of kalam as a communal obligation (fard kifaya) rather than a personal one, and how this ruling balances necessity with practicality.

Core Idea
Kalam is a communal shield against doubt, not a personal need for all.

Imam al-Ghazali now addresses a crucial practical question: who must study kalam? He has already established that firm belief is sufficient for the common person. But that does not mean kalam can be abandoned. In this passage, he explains why it must be pursued by some in every community, and what happens if it is neglected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the structure: thesis, proof, objection, answer, and conclusion.
Pay attention to the key terms fard 'ayn and fard kifaya.
2
Identify the two categories of obligation
Look for the terms fard 'ayn and fard kifaya. Understand what each means and how Ghazali distinguishes them.
3
Follow the objection and its response
The objector questions why kalam should be obligatory if most are not harmed. See how Ghazali responds with three reasons.
List the reasons: removing doubts, calling to truth, countering innovators.
4
Consider the analogy with the physician and jurist
Reflect on the final comparison to understand the communal responsibility. What does the absence of such a person imply?

التمهيد الثالث
في بيان أن الاشتغال بهذا العلم من فروض الكفايات
اعلم : أنَّ التبحرَ في هذا العلم والاشتغال بمجامعه ليس من فروض الأعيان ، بل هو من فروض الكفايات
فأما أنَّه ليس من فروض الأعيان.. فقد اتضح لك برهانه في التمهيد (۱)؛ إذ تبيَّنَ أنَّه ليس يجب على كافة الخلق إلا التصديق الجازم وتطهير القلب عن الريب والشك في الإيمان ، وإنَّما تصيرُ إزالة الشك فرضَ عين في حق من اعتراه الشك
فإن قلت : فلِمَ صار من فروض الكفايات ، وقد ذكرت أن أكثر الفرق يضرُّهم ذلك ولا ينفعهم ؟
فاعلم : أنَّه قد سبق أنَّ إزالة الشكوك في أصول العقائد واجبةٌ ، واعتوار الشك غير مستحيل وإن كان لا يقع إلا في الأقل ، ثم الدعوة إلى الحق بالبرهان لمن هو مصر على الباطل ، ومحتمل بذكائه لفهم البراهين .. مهم
ثم لا يبعد أن يثور مبتدع ويتصدى لإغواء أهل الحق بإفاضة الشبهة فيهم ، فلا بد ممَّنْ يقاوم شبهته بالكشف ، ويعارض إغواءه بالتقبيح
ولا يمكن ذلك إلا بالعلم ، ولا تنفكُ البلاد عن أمثال هذه الوقائع ، فوجب أن يكون في كل قطر من الأقطار وصقع من الأصقاع قائم بالحقِّ ، مشتغل بهذا العلم ، يقاوم دعاة المبتدعة ، ويستميلُ المائلين عن الحقِّ ، ويصفي قلوب أهل السنة عن عوارض الشبهة ، فلو خلا عنه القطر .. حَرِجَ به أهلُ القطر كافة ؛ كما لو خلا عن الطبيب والفقيه
The Third Preliminary: On clarifying that engaging in this science is from the communal obligations. Know that deep immersion in this science and occupying oneself with its comprehensive matters is not from the personal obligations, but rather it is from the communal obligations. As for it not being a personal obligation, its proof has become clear to you in the second preliminary, since it was made clear that only firm belief and purification of the heart from doubt and uncertainty in faith are obligatory upon all of creation, and removing doubt only becomes a personal obligation in the case of one whom doubt has befallen. If you say: Why then has it become a communal obligation, when you have mentioned that most sects are not harmed by that and it does not benefit them? Then know: It has preceded that removing doubts in the principles of beliefs is obligatory, and the occurrence of doubt is not impossible even if it only happens in the minority. Then calling to the truth with proof for one who is persistent upon falsehood and likely, due to his intelligence, to understand the proofs, is important. Then it is not far-fetched that an innovator may arise and set out to seduce the people of truth by pouring doubt upon them. Thus there must be someone who resists his doubt with disclosure and opposes his seduction with condemnation. And that is not possible except through knowledge, and lands are not free from such incidents. So it is necessary that in every region and every district there be one who upholds the truth, occupied with this science, resisting the callers of innovation, attracting those who incline away from the truth, and purifying the hearts of the people of the Sunnah from the intrusions of doubt. For if the region were empty of him, all the people of the region would be in sin, just as if it were empty of a physician and a jurist.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage begins the third preliminary, in which Ghazali establishes the ruling that engaging in the science of kalam is a communal obligation (fard kifaya), not a personal one (fard 'ayn). He first states the thesis: deep study of kalam is not individually obligatory. The proof for this, he says, was given in the second preliminary: only firm belief and purity from doubt are required of everyone; removing doubt is obligatory only on those who experience it. Then he anticipates an objection: if most people are not harmed by neglecting kalam, why is it obligatory at all? His answer is that removing doubts in creedal foundations is necessary, and doubt can occur even if rarely. Moreover, calling people to the truth with proof is important, and innovators may arise who spread doubts. Therefore, there must always be someone qualified to counter them, and this requires knowledge of kalam. Hence, every region must have such a person; otherwise, all inhabitants become sinful—just as a town without a physician or jurist would be at fault.

Key Terms
فروض الكفايات
فروض الأعيان
التصديق الجازم
إزالة الشك
مبتدع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam is optional because it is not a personal obligation.
Correction: Ghazali explains that it is a communal obligation, so if no one in a region engages in it, the whole community is sinful.

Checkpoint

A) Every believer without exception
B) Only scholars
C) One whom doubt has befallen
D) No one, it is always communal

Key Takeaway
Kalam is a communal obligation: every region must have someone capable of defending the faith against doubts and innovations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your local community, is there someone who can defend the faith against doubts about Allah or the prophets? If not, are you content to let that burden fall on others, or will you take steps to learn kalam yourself? What is one concrete action you can take today to start building that defense?
?
Ghazali says that if a region lacks a kalam scholar, all the people of that region are sinful. When is the last time you felt any sense of responsibility for the spiritual vulnerability of your neighbors? Does their faith matter to you enough to seek knowledge that could shield them?
?
You have heard arguments today that dismiss Islam—online, in conversations, or in media. Did you feel helpless because you could not respond? How does Ghazali’s teaching expose your own negligence? Will you continue to rely on others, or will you dedicate yourself to learning the proofs so that you are not a cause of sin for your community?

Why Fiqh Takes Priority Over Kalam
Ghazali compares kalam to fiqh and medicine, prioritizing fiqh, and refutes a physician's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's practical argument for prioritizing fiqh over kalam when both are needed in a community, and his refutation of objections from kalam proponents and physicians.

Core Idea
In practice, fiqh is more urgent than kalam because everyone needs it daily.

Ghazali presents a concrete scenario: a person capable of studying either fiqh or kalam, but a community lacking both. Which should he choose? This lesson unpacks Ghazali's reasoning, including analogies with medicine and responses to objections, to show the practical priority of fiqh for the common good.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali sets up the scenario and gives his ruling.
2
Examine the medicine analogy
Identify why Ghazali compares fiqh to medicine. Note that everyone needs fiqh, while only the sick need medicine.
The analogy highlights universality versus rarity.
3
Consider the 'root vs branch' objection
Ghazali acknowledges that kalam is the root but argues that correct belief can be attained through imitation, making the need for kalam rare.
This shows Ghazali's nuanced approach.
4
Refute the physician's claim
The physician argues health is prerequisite for religion. Ghazali dismisses this as obfuscation. Ask yourself why this argument fails.
Think about the purpose of life beyond health.

نعم ؛ من أَنِسَ من نفسه تعلُّم الفقه أو الكلام ، وشَغَر الصقع عن القائم بهما ، ولم يتسع زمانه للجمع بينهما ، واستفتى في تعيين ما يشتغل به .. أوجبنا عليه الاشتغال بالفقه ؛ فإن الحاجة إليه أعم ، والوقائع فيه أكثر ، فلا يستغني أحد في ليله ونهاره عن الاستعانة بالفقه ، واعتوار الشكوك المُخوجة إلى علم الكلام نادر بالإضافة إليه ، كما أنه لو خلا البلد الطبيب والفقيه .. كان الاشتغال بالفقه أهم ؛ لأنَّه يشترك في الحاجة إليه الجماهير والدهماء ، وأما الطب .. فلا يحتاج إليه الأصحاء ، والمرضى أقل عدداً بالإضافة إليهم ، ثم المريضُ لا يستغني عن الفقه كما لا يستغني عن الطب ، وحاجته إلى الطب لحياته الفانية ، وإلى الفقه لحياته الباقية وشتان ما بين الحياتين
فإذا نسبت ثمرة الطب إلى ثمرة الفقه .. علمت أن ما بين المَثْمرين ما بين الثمرتين ، ويدلُّكَ على أن الفقه أهم العلوم ؛ لاشتغال الصحابة بالبحث عنه في مشاوراتهم ومفاوضاتهم ، ولا يغرنَّكَ ما يهولُ به من يعظم صناعة الكلام من أنَّه الأصل ، والفقه فرع له ؛ فإنَّها كلمة حق ، ولكنها غير نافعة في هذا المقام
فإنّ الأصل هو الاعتقاد الصحيح والتصديق الجزم ، وذلك حاصل بالتقليد ، والحاجة إلى البرهان ودقائق الجدل نادرة ، والطبيب أيضاً قد يلبس فيقول : وجودُكَ ووجود بدنك موقوف على صناعتي ، وحياتك منوطة بي ، فالحياة والصحة أولاً ، ثم الاشتغال بالدين .
ولكن لا يخفى ما تحت هذا الكلام من التمويه ، وقد نبهنا عليه
Yes, one who feels in himself the capacity to learn fiqh or kalam, and the region is empty of one who undertakes them both, and his time does not extend to combine them, and he seeks a ruling on which to occupy himself with—we would obligate him to occupy himself with fiqh, for the need for it is more general, and the occurrences in it are more numerous. No one is free day and night from seeking the help of fiqh, while the occurrence of doubts that lead to the science of kalam is rare in comparison. Similarly, if a town is empty of a physician and a jurist, occupying oneself with fiqh is more important because the masses and common people share in the need for it. As for medicine, healthy people do not need it, and the sick are fewer in number compared to them. Then the sick person is not free from need of fiqh just as he is not free from need of medicine. His need for medicine is for his ephemeral life, and for fiqh for his everlasting life—and how great is the difference between the two lives! So if you compare the fruit of medicine to the fruit of fiqh, you know that the difference between the two fruits is like the difference between the two fruits. And it indicates to you that fiqh is the most important of sciences, because the Companions occupied themselves with researching it in their consultations and discussions. And do not let yourself be deceived by what those who magnify the craft of kalam may terrify with, that it is the root and fiqh is a branch of it. For that is a true statement, but it is not beneficial in this context. For the root is correct belief and firm conviction, and that is attained by imitation, and the need for proof and the subtleties of debate is rare. And the physician also might equivocate and say: Your existence and the existence of your body depend on my craft, and your life is tied to me, so life and health come first, then occupying oneself with religion. But what is hidden beneath this speech of obfuscation is not concealed, and we have drawn attention to it.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the relative importance of kalam. Ghazali addresses a practical scenario: if a person is capable of learning either fiqh or kalam, and a community lacks both a faqih and a mutakallim, and the person cannot study both, which should he choose? Ghazali rules that fiqh takes priority because the need for it is more universal—everyone needs fiqh daily, whereas doubts requiring kalam are rare. He draws an analogy with a town lacking both a physician and a faqih: fiqh is more important because everyone needs it, while only the sick need medicine. Furthermore, the benefit of medicine is for this temporary life, while fiqh benefits the eternal life. He then refutes an argument from those who exalt kalam by claiming it is the root (asl) and fiqh a branch. Ghazali concedes the statement is true—correct belief is the root—but argues that correct belief can be attained through taqlid (imitation) without delving into kalam's proofs. The need for proof is rare. Finally, he counters a potential objection from a physician who might claim that physical life and health are prerequisites for religious practice, implying medicine is more fundamental. Ghazali dismisses this as obfuscation (tamwih), having already addressed it.

Key Terms
الفقه
الكلام
الطب
التقليد
التمويه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think kalam is always more important than fiqh because it deals with foundational beliefs.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that while kalam is root in theory, in practice fiqh is more urgent because its need is universal and constant, and correct belief can be attained through imitation.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does he obligate a person capable of studying either fiqh or kalam to choose fiqh when the community lacks both?
A) Because kalam is forbidden
B) Because fiqh is more prestigious
C) Because the need for fiqh is more general and its occurrences more frequent
D) Because the Prophet commanded it

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that fiqh takes practical precedence over kalam because it serves a universal and constant need, while the need for kalam is rare; moreover, foundational beliefs can be acquired through imitation without intricate proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are faced with a choice between studying a practical religious obligation and an intellectual theological debate, which do you instinctively lean toward? What does that reveal about the state of your heart?
?
You claim to believe in the Hereafter, yet do you devote more time to securing your ephemeral health and wealth than to learning what will save you in your eternal life? Where is your real investment?
?
Al-Ghazali says the need for kalam is rare. Have you ever used the excuse of seeking deeper knowledge to avoid the simpler, daily duties that Allah has already made clear to you? Will you repent from this procrastination today?

The Priority of Fiqh over Kalam
Ghazali argues for the priority of fiqh over kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Ghazali, fiqh is more crucial for the general Muslim than kalam, and how he addresses counterarguments.

Core Idea
Fiqh is more important than kalam because everyone needs it daily.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a practical question: if a person can only study one Islamic science, which should it be? He argues for fiqh (jurisprudence) over kalam (theology), citing the universal and frequent need for fiqh in daily life. He reinforces his point with an analogy comparing the roles of a jurist and a physician, and then refutes the objection that kalam is foundational by noting that sound belief can be attained through imitation without deep theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow of Ghazali's argument from the initial scenario to the concluding dismissal of objections.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify Ghazali's main argument
Find the core reason Ghazali gives for prioritizing fiqh. He emphasizes that the need for fiqh is 'more general' and 'more frequent' than the need for kalam.
3
Examine the medicine-physician analogy
How does the analogy between fiqh and medicine support his argument? Notice that everyone needs fiqh, while only the sick need medicine. This highlights the universality of fiqh.
Also note the contrast between eternal and perishable life.
4
Understand his refutation of objections
Ghazali addresses the claim that kalam is the origin and fiqh is merely a branch. He agrees in principle, but argues that the need for kalam's proofs is rare. Sound belief can be achieved through taqlid.
5
Reflect on the physician's argument
The physician's counter-argument that physical life precedes religion is dismissed as obfuscation. Consider why Ghazali finds it unconvincing.
This step ties the whole lesson together.

Yes; whoever finds in himself the ability to learn fiqh or kalam, and the place is empty of one who is engaged in fiqh in a town devoid of a jurist in both, and his time does not allow combining them, and he seeks a fatwa regarding which to occupy himself with, we oblige him to occupy himself with fiqh. For the need for it is more general, and the occurences in which there is need for it are more frequent. No one is independent in his night or day from seeking help from fiqh, while the occurrence of doubts that lead to the science of kalam is rare in comparison. Similarly, if a town lacks a physician and a jurist, occupying oneself with fiqh is more important because the masses and the common people share in the need for it. As for medicine, the healthy do not need it, and the sick are fewer in number compared to them. Then the sick person is not independent of fiqh just as he is not independent of medicine. His need for medicine is for his perishable life, and for fiqh for his eternal life. How great is the difference between the two lives! So if you compare the fruit of medicine to the fruit of fiqh, you know that the difference between the two fruits is like the difference between the two fruits. And it indicates to you that fiqh is the most important of the sciences because the Companions occupied themselves with researching it in their consultations and negotiations. Do not be deceived by what those who magnify the craft of kalam terrify with, saying that the origin is sound belief, and that kalam is the origin and fiqh is a branch of it. For it is a true word, but it is not beneficial in this context. For the origin is correct belief and firm affirmation, and that is attained by taqlid (imitation), and the need for demonstration and subtleties of dialectic is rare. The physician also may confuse, saying: 'Your existence and the existence of your body depend on my craft, and your life is entrusted to me, so life and health first, then occupation with religion.' But what is hidden in this speech of obfuscation is not hidden, and we have pointed it out.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a comparative argument establishing the priority of fiqh (Islamic jurisprudence) over kalam (dialectical theology) for those who must choose. Ghazali presents the scenario of a person who can learn only one, and rules that fiqh is obligatory because its need is more general and frequent. He reinforces this with an analogy: if a town lacks both a physician and a jurist, the jurist is more important because everyone needs fiqh (guidance on daily acts) while only the sick need medicine. Furthermore, one's need for fiqh extends to eternal life, whereas medicine is only for this perishable life. He then refutes the objection that kalam is the origin because it provides sound belief, by noting that sound belief can be attained by taqlid (imitation) without demonstration, so the need for kalam's proofs is rare. Finally, he dismisses the physician's counter-argument that physical life precedes religion as an obfuscation.

الكلام (kalam)
التقليد (taqlid)
البرهان (burhan)

Misconception: Some think kalam is always more important because it deals with foundational beliefs of faith.
Correction: Ghazali explains that sound belief can be obtained through taqlid (imitation) without deep theological proofs, and the need for kalam arises only for rare doubts, so fiqh takes priority due to daily necessity.

A) Because fiqh is easier to learn.
B) Because the need for fiqh is more general and frequent.
C) Because kalam is forbidden.
D) Because the Companions abandoned kalam.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali prioritizes fiqh over kalam due to its universal and frequent need, and he dismisses objections by noting that sound belief can be attained by imitation.

?

The Method of Survey and Division (al-Sabr wa al-Taqsim)
Ghazali defines the method of survey and division with example and logical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of al-sabr wa al-taqsim and how it yields certain knowledge in kalam arguments.

Core Idea
Survey and division reduces two possibilities to one by eliminating the impossible.

Ghazali introduces a key logical tool used throughout Islamic theology: the method of survey and division. Before diving into doctrinal proofs, he wants the reader to grasp how a proper rational argument is structured. This passage defines the method and gives a classic example, then explains the necessary conditions for such an argument to produce knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm and structure come through before you interpret.
Notice how Ghazali contrasts this method with obscure paths.
2
Identify the two divisions and their role
The method works by restricting a matter to exactly two possibilities, then nullifying one. Identify these in the example of the world being eternal or originated.
The divisions must be exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
3
Understand the logical condition for knowledge production
Ghazali emphasises that the two premisses must be coupled in a specific way. Ask yourself: why can't any two premisses produce knowledge?
This guards against a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التمهيد الرابع في بيان مناهج الأدلة التي استهجناها في هذا الكتاب اعلم : أنَّ مناهج الأدلة متشعبة ، وقد أوردنا بعضها في كتاب « محك النظر » ، وأشبعنا القول فيها في كتاب ( معيار العلم ) ، ولكنّا في هذا الكتاب نحترز عن الطرق المنغلقة والمسالك الغامضة ؛ قصداً للإيضاح والتقسيم وميلاً إلى الإيجاز ، واجتناباً للتطويل ونقتصر على ثلاثة مناهج : المنهج الأول : تعريف وتمثيل السبر والتقسيم (۱) : وهو أن نحصر الأمر في قسمين ، ثم نبطل أحدهما ، فنعلم منه ثبوت الثاني ؛ كقولنا : العالم : إما حادث ، وإما قديم ، ومحال أن يكون قديماً ؛ فلزم منه أن يكون حادثاً لا محالة ، وهذا اللازم هو مطلوبنا ، وهو علم مقصود استفدناه من علمين آخرين أحدهما : قولنا : العالم : إما قديم ، وإما حادث ، فإن الحكم بهذا الانحصار علم . والثاني : قولنا : ومحال أن يكون قديماً ، فإن هذا علم آخرُ والثالث : هو اللازم منهما ، وهو المطلوب : أنه حادث شرط إنتاج العلم وكلُّ علم مطلوب : فلا يمكن أن يستفاد إلا من علمين هما أصلان ولا كل أصلين (۲) ؛ بل إذا وقع بينهما ازدواج على وجه مخصوص وشرط
The Fourth Preliminary: On explaining the methods of proofs that we have adopted in this book. Know that the methods of proofs are branched. We have mentioned some of them in the book 'Mihakk al-Nazar', and we have spoken fully about them in the book 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm'. But in this book we avoid the blocked paths and obscure passages, intending clarification and division, and inclining toward brevity, and avoiding length. We confine ourselves to three methods: The First Method: The definition and example of survey and division: It is that we restrict the matter to two divisions, then we nullify one of them, so we learn from it the affirmation of the second. Like our saying: The world: either originated or eternal, and it is impossible for it to be eternal; so it necessarily follows that it is originated. This necessary consequence is our desired conclusion, and it is a sought-after knowledge that we have derived from two other knowledges: one of them is our saying: The world: either eternal or originated; for the judgment of this restriction is a knowledge. The second is our saying: And it is impossible for it to be eternal; for this is another knowledge. And the third is the necessary consequence from them, which is the desired conclusion: that it is originated. The condition for producing knowledge: Every sought-after knowledge cannot be derived except from two knowledges that are principles, nor from just any two principles; rather, when there occurs between them a coupling in a specific manner and condition.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to a methodological section on proofs. Ghazali begins by stating that the methods of proofs are many, but he will only mention three, aiming for clarity and brevity. The first method is 'al-sabr wa al-taqsim' (survey and division). He defines it as restricting the matter to two possibilities, then invalidating one, thereby proving the other. He illustrates with a classic kalam argument: the world is either originated (hadith) or eternal (qadim); it is impossible for it to be eternal; therefore it is originated. He then analyzes the logical structure: this conclusion is a new piece of knowledge derived from two premisses: (1) the exhaustive division (the world is either hadith or qadim), and (2) the invalidation of one (the world cannot be qadim). The third element is the necessary conclusion. He ends by stating a condition: every sought-after knowledge must come from two principles, but not just any two; they must be related in a specific way (a coupling). The passage breaks off, but the gist is clear.

Key Terms
السبر والتقسيم (al-sabr wa al-taqsim)
المنهج (method)
القسمان (two divisions)
القديم (eternal)
الحادث (originated)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two true statements can be combined to produce new knowledge.
Correction: Ghazali specifies that the two premisses must be coupled in a specific manner (ازدواج على وجه مخصوص) – they must form an exhaustive division and a valid negation.

Checkpoint
In Ghazali's example of al-sabr wa al-taqsim, what two premisses lead to the conclusion that the world is originated?
A) The world is eternal; the world is originated.
B) The world is either eternal or originated; it is impossible for it to be eternal.
C) The world is created; the Creator exists.
D) The world is one; the world has a cause.

Key Takeaway
The method of survey and division (al-sabr wa al-taqsim) produces certain knowledge by combining an exhaustive division with the elimination of one option, provided the two premisses are properly coupled.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult decision, do you consciously list all possible options and eliminate the false ones, or do you settle for the most comfortable answer? Which approach aligns with the method of survey and division?
?
The passage says knowledge comes from two premisses properly coupled. Think of a conviction you hold strongly. Are its foundations two clear, true premisses rightly connected? Or do you hold it without such rigorous scrutiny?
?
Is there a belief you refuse to examine because its elimination would force you to accept a truth you find uncomfortable? If you applied the method of survey and division honestly, what would you have to conclude?

The First Method: Enumeration and Elimination
Ghazali defines and illustrates the first proof method: enumeration and elimination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first method of proof (enumeration and elimination) and how it yields necessary knowledge from two premises under a specific condition.

Core Idea
Limit to two options, eliminate one, and the other necessarily holds.

This lesson presents the first of three proof methods that Ghazali uses in the Iqtisad. He begins by noting that proofs can be complex, but here he simplifies. His first method, called 'enumeration and elimination,' is a powerful logical tool: you restrict a matter to two exclusive possibilities, then prove one false, so the other must be true. Through an example—the world's creation—Ghazali demonstrates how two premises combine under a specific condition to produce a certain conclusion. This method is foundational for the theological arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: an introduction, a definition, an example, and a condition.
Pay attention to the technical terms: السبر والتقسيم, الأصلان, الدعوى, المطلوب.
2
Identify the three methods
Ghazali mentions he will restrict himself to three methods. This lesson focuses on the first method. Understand its definition: 'hasting an affair into two divisions, then invalidating one, so we know the other.'
Capture the phrase: حصر الأمر في قسمين، ثم إبطال أحدهما.
3
Walk through the example
Follow the example of the world's createdness: the world is either eternal or originated; it cannot be eternal; therefore it is originated. Notice how this yields three knowledges.
Identify the two premises and the conclusion.
4
Understand the condition
Ghazali stresses that not any two premises produce knowledge; they must be paired in a specific manner and condition. Reflect on what that condition might entail.
The condition is that the pairing must be ‘a specific manner and a specific condition’.
5
Learn the terminology
The conclusion can be called a 'claim' (da‘wā), 'sought' (maṭlūb), 'benefit' (fā’ida), or 'branch' (far‘) depending on context. Understand the relational terms.
The premises are called 'principles' (aṣlān) and the conclusion is derived from them.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أنَّ مناهج الأدلة متشعبة ، وقد أوردنا بعضها في كتاب « محك النظر »، وأشبعنا القول فيها في كتاب ( معيار العلم ) ، ولكنّا في هذا الكتاب نحترز عن الطرق المنغلقة والمسالك الغامضة ؛ قصداً للإيضاح والتقسيم وميلاً إلى الإيجاز ، واجتناباً للتطويل . ونقتصر على ثلاثة مناهج :
المنهج الأول : تعريف وتمثيل السبر والتقسيم (۱) : وهو أن نحصر الأمر في قسمين ، ثم نبطل أحدهما ، فنعلم منه ثبوت الثاني ؛ كقولنا : العالم : إما حادث ، وإما قديم ، ومحال أن يكون قديماً ؛ فلزم منه أن يكون حادثاً لا محالة ، وهذا اللازم هو مطلوبنا ، وهو علم مقصود استفدناه من علمين آخرين أحدهما : قولنا : العالم : إما قديم ، وإما حادث ، فإن الحكم بهذا الانحصار علم . والثاني : قولنا : ومحال أن يكون قديماً ، فإن هذا علم آخرُ . والثالث : هو اللازم منهما ، وهو المطلوب : أنه حادث . وكلُّ علم مطلوب : فلا يمكن أن يستفاد إلا من علمين هما أصلان ولا كل أصلين (۲) ؛ بل إذا وقع بينهما ازدواج على وجه مخصوص وشرط مخصوص ، فإذا وقع الازدواج على شرطه أفاد علماً ثالثاً ، وهو المطلوب ، وهذا الثالث قد نسميه دعوى إذا كان لنا خصم ، ونسميه مطلوباً إذا لم يكن لنا خصم ؛ لأنه مطلبُ الناظر ، ونسميه فائدة وفرعاً بالإضافة إلى الأصلين ؛ فإنه مستفاد منهما ، ومهما أقرَّ الخصم بالأصلين . . يلزمه - لا محالة - الإقرار بالفرع المستفاد منهما ، وهو صحة الدعوى .
Know that the paths of proofs are branched, and we have presented some of them in the book 'The Criterion of Reasoning' (Miḥakk al-Naẓar), and we have fully discussed them in the book 'The Standard of Knowledge' (Miʿyār al-ʿIlm), but in this book we are cautious of closed paths and obscure ways, aiming for clarification and division, and inclining toward brevity, and avoiding prolixity. We restrict ourselves to three paths: The first path: Definition and illustration of enumeration and elimination (al-sabr wa-l-taqsīm): It is that we restrict the matter to two divisions, then we invalidate one of them, so we know from that the establishment of the second; as our saying: The world is either originated (ḥādith) or eternal (qadīm), and it is impossible that it be eternal; so it necessarily follows that it is originated. This necessary consequence is our sought (maṭlūb), and it is a knowledge we have derived from two other knowledges: one of them is our saying: The world is either eternal or originated, for the judgment of this exhaustive division is a knowledge. The second is our saying: And it is impossible that it be eternal, for this is another knowledge. And the third is the necessary consequence from them, which is the sought: that it is originated. And every sought knowledge cannot be derived except from two knowledges that are two principles (aṣlān), and not just any two principles; rather, only when a pairing occurs between them in a specific manner and a specific condition. When the pairing occurs under its condition, it yields a third knowledge, which is the sought. And this third we may call a claim (daʿwā) if we have an opponent, and we call it a sought (maṭlūb) if we have no opponent because it is the seeker's aim, and we call it a benefit (fāʾida) and a branch (farʿ) in relation to the two principles, for it is derived from them. And whenever the opponent acknowledges the two principles, he is necessarily compelled to acknowledge the branch derived from them, which is the validity of the claim.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the three methods of proof used in this book, followed by a detailed explanation of the first method, known as 'enumeration and elimination' (السبر والتقسيم). The passage begins by noting that the paths of proofs are many, and Ghazali has treated them in other works, but in this book he restricts himself to three clear and concise methods. The first method is defined: one restricts the matter to two exclusive possibilities, then disproves one, establishing the other. The example given is the proof of the world's createdness: the world is either eternal or originated; it cannot be eternal, therefore it is originated. This yields a third knowledge derived from two premises: the exhaustive division (the world is either eternal or originated) and the impossibility of one premise (it cannot be eternal). Ghazali then explains the condition for productive reasoning: not any two premises produce knowledge, but only when they are paired in a specific way. The resulting knowledge is called a claim, a sought, a benefit, or a branch depending on context, and if the opponent accepts both premises, he must accept the conclusion.

Key Terms
المناهج
السبر والتقسيم
الأصلان
الدعوى
المطلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two premises can produce a valid conclusion.
Correction: Ghazali stresses that premises must be paired in a specific manner and condition to yield necessary knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the essential condition for two premises to produce a third knowledge?
A) They must be about the world
B) They must be acknowledged by the opponent
C) They must be paired in a specific manner and condition
D) They must be simple statements

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first method of proof—enumeration and elimination—demonstrates how two premises, when properly paired, necessarily yield a third truth, providing a reliable foundation for theological reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali says that if an opponent acknowledges the two principles (al-aṣlān), he must necessarily accept the branch (al-farʿ). In your own life, do you acknowledge the clear principles of faith (e.g., Allah's existence, the truth of the Quran) but resist the necessary conclusions—like the obligation to pray, give charity, or abandon a particular sin? Which conclusion are you withholding allegiance from, and what is the real reason you haven't submitted? Commit to one concrete action to align your life with that truth today.
?
Enumeration and elimination requires that the two options be truly exhaustive and the elimination be based on impossibility, not preference. Think of a major decision you are currently facing (e.g., a career choice, a relationship, a habit to break). Have you honestly restricted the matter to two real possibilities, or are you avoiding a clear-cut conclusion by adding false options? What would it mean to rule out the wrong path as 'impossible' in light of your ultimate goal—pleasing Allah? Write down the two options and the evidence that eliminates one, then take a step toward the remaining path.
?
The passage teaches that knowledge (al-ʿilm) is derived from two premises paired under a specific condition. In your pursuit of Islamic knowledge, do you merely accumulate information, or do you actively seek to pair the principles you learn (e.g., the names of Allah with their implications, the rulings with their wisdoms) so that they yield transformative conclusions? What is one area where you have the right premises but have not yet produced the 'sought knowledge' that changes how you live? Decide on one pairing to work on this week.

The Second Method: Arranging Two Premises
Ghazali explains the syllogistic method of proof with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali's second method of proof uses two premises in a syllogism to derive a necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
Two premises, one conclusion: if premises are granted, the conclusion is inescapable.

Ghazali now presents a second method of proof that is simpler and more direct than the first. Instead of exhaustive division, he uses a syllogistic arrangement of two premises to demonstrate the world's createdness. This method highlights the logical necessity of the conclusion when the premises are accepted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure of the argument.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the two statements Ghazali calls 'principles'. What are they? How do they combine to yield the conclusion?
The first premise is a general rule; the second is a specific fact about the world.
3
Reflect on the logical necessity
Consider Ghazali's concluding question: can an opponent accept both premises and deny the conclusion? Why is that impossible? This underscores the force of syllogistic reasoning.
This method shows that rational argument can compel assent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنهج الثاني : أن نرتب أصلين على وجه آخرَ : مثل قولنا : كلُّ ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث ، وهو أصل ، والعالم لا يخلو عن الحوادث ، وهو أصل آخرُ ؛ فيلزم منه صحة دعوانا وهو أن العالم حادث ، وهو المطلوب . فتأمل : هل يتصوَّرُ أن يقرَّ الخصمُ بالأصلين ثم يمكنه إنكار صحة الدعوى ؟ فتعلم قطعاً أن ذلك محال .
The second path: That we arrange two principles in another manner: such as our saying: Everything that is not free of originated things is itself originated, that is a principle; and the world is not free of originated things, that is another principle; then from that follows the validity of our claim that the world is originated, which is the sought. So reflect: Is it conceivable that the opponent acknowledges the two principles and then be able to deny the validity of the claim? Then you know for certain that this is impossible.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the second method of proof. Unlike the first method which relies on exhaustive division and elimination, this method uses two premises in a syllogistic form. The example is the argument for the world's createdness: (1) Whatever is not free from originated things is itself originated, and (2) The world is not free from originated things. Therefore, the world is originated. Ghazali then emphasizes that if the opponent accepts both premises, he cannot logically deny the conclusion. This highlights the necessity of the inference.

Key Terms
المنهج الثاني
أصلان
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He may still deny the conclusion
B) He must accept the conclusion necessarily
C) He must provide a counter-premise
D) He can reject the method

Key Takeaway
The second method of proof is a syllogism: if two premises are accepted, the conclusion follows necessarily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Third Method: Proving Impossibility of Opponent's Claim
Ghazali introduces the third method: proving opponent's claim leads to impossibility, with an example about infinite celestial cycles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's third method of proof, which demonstrates the impossibility of the opponent's position by showing it leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Reductio ad absurdum: prove opponent false by showing their claim leads to impossibility.

Ghazali presents a powerful dialectical tool. Instead of directly proving his own view, he shows that the opponent's claim inevitably leads to an impossibility. This method, known as reductio ad absurdum, is central to kalam argumentation. The following passage explains the method and provides an example concerning the infinity of celestial cycles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully.
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note how Ghazali introduces the method and then gives an example.
The passage cuts off near the end; focus on the introduced method and example.
2
Identify the method's key components.
Notice that Ghazali says he does not try to prove his own claim, but instead shows that the opponent's claim leads to something impossible (المحال). The rule: 'whatever leads to the impossible is itself impossible.'
3
Analyze the example provided.
The example: if the cycles of the celestial sphere are infinite (no end), then it would follow that something infinite has come to an end – which is impossible. Therefore, the claim of infinite cycles is impossible.
The opponent's claim leads to a contradiction, thus is refuted.
4
Reflect on the dialectical strength.
Consider why this method is effective: it relies on the opponent's own premises without assuming one's own position. It forces the opponent to accept the impossibility of their own claim.
This is not circular reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنهج الثالث : الا نتعرض لثبوت دعوانا ، بل ندعي استحالة دعوى الخصم : بأن نبين أنَّه مفض إلى المحال ، وما يفضي إلى المحال فهو محال لا محالة . مثاله : قولنا : إن صح قول الخصم : إنَّ دوراتِ الفَلَكِ لا نهاية لها .. لزم منه صحة قول القائل : إنَّ ما لا نهاية له قد انقضى وفرغ منه ، ومعلوم أن هذا اللازم محال ؛ فيلزم منه أن المفضَى إليه محال ، وهو مذهب الخصم . فههنا أصلان : أحدهما : قولنا : إن كانت دورات الفلك لا نهاية لها .. فقد انقضى ما لا نهاية له ؛ فإن الحكم بلزوم انقضاء ما لا نهاية له على القول بنفي النهاية عن دورات الفلك . . علم ندعيه ونحكم به، يُتصوَّرُ فيه من الخصم إقرار حتم
The third path: We do not undertake to prove our claim, but rather we claim the impossibility of the opponent's claim: by showing that it leads to the impossible, and whatever leads to the impossible is itself impossible without doubt. An example: Our saying: If the opponent's statement that the cycles of the celestial sphere have no end is true, then it follows the validity of the statement that what has no end has come to an end and finished, and it is known that this necessary consequence is impossible; so it follows that what leads to it is impossible, which is the opponent's position. So here there are two principles: One of them is our saying: If the cycles of the celestial sphere have no end, then what has no end has come to an end; for the judgment of the necessity of the termination of what has no end based on the claim of no end for the cycles of the celestial sphere... a knowledge we claim and judge, in which the opponent's acknowledgment is inevitable (ḥatm).
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage introduces the third method of proof, which is a reductio ad absurdum. Instead of proving one's own claim directly, one shows that the opponent's claim leads to an impossibility, thereby proving the opponent's claim false. The example concerns the eternal cycles of the celestial sphere. If the opponent claims the cycles are infinite (no end), then it would necessitate that something infinite has come to an end, which is impossible. Therefore, the opponent's claim is impossible. Ghazali then begins to analyze this into two principles, but the passage cuts off. The available text indicates that one principle is the relation between infinite cycles and their termination, and the opponent's acknowledgment is inevitable.

Key Terms
المنهج الثالث
المحال
مفض إلى المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This method is circular because it assumes what it wants to prove.
Correction: It is not circular because it relies only on the opponent's own premises and does not assume one's own claim. The reductio shows that the opponent's position leads to an absurdity, thereby refuting it.

Checkpoint
In the third method, what does Ghazali aim to prove?
A) The truth of his own claim directly
B) That the opponent's claim leads to an impossibility
C) That the world is eternal
D) That proofs are unnecessary

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's third method refutes an opponent by showing their claim leads to an impossibility, without needing to prove one's own view directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, when you thought it through, led to a contradiction you avoided facing? What would it cost you to honestly apply Ghazali's method to that belief today?
?
Consider a time you defended your position in a debate. Were you more focused on winning or on avoiding logical inconsistency? Are you willing to let your own claims be tested by this method?
?
Ghazali's example involves an infinite past. If you believe the universe has a beginning, does your belief rely on a logical proof or on faith? Would you be willing to abandon it if shown it leads to impossibility?

The First Method: Combining Two Premises to Produce a Conclusion
Ghazali defines the first method of deduction and its terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first method of reasoning in kalam: how pairing two premises under the right condition necessarily yields a third knowledge, and how that conclusion is named depending on the context.

Core Idea
A valid deduction forces assent: acknowledge the premises, and you must accept the conclusion.

Imam al-Ghazali begins his discussion of reasoning methods by defining the most basic form: combining two premises to derive a new piece of knowledge. This method is the foundation of all theological argumentation. In this passage, he explains the key terms and conditions for such a deduction to be valid, and how the resulting knowledge is referred to in different situations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the literal translation. Try to grasp the meaning of the key terms: الازدواج, الأصلان, المطلوب, الدعوى, الفائدة, الفرع.
Notice the conditional phrase 'على شرطه' – the pairing must meet its condition.
2
Identify the components of the method
The passage describes the process: two premises (أصلان) are paired under a condition, producing a third knowledge. Identify the different names given to this third knowledge and why they vary.
The names depend on whether there is an opponent or not.
3
Understand the logical necessity
Ghazali emphasizes that if the opponent accepts the two premises, he must necessarily accept the derived conclusion. This is the core of deductive validity.
This is a key principle in kalam: valid reasoning compels assent.
4
Consider the common misunderstanding
Reflect on the misconception that any two statements yield a conclusion. Ghazali's condition ('على شرطه') shows that the pairing must be properly structured.
The condition is not specified here but will be detailed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا وقع الازدواج على شرطه أفاد علماً ثالثاً، وهو المطلوب، وهذا الثالث قد نسميه دعوى إذا كان لنا خصم، ونسميه مطلوباً أسماء العلم الناتج إذا لم يكن لنا خصم؛ لأنه مطلبُ الناظر، ونسميه فائدة وفرعاً بالإضافة إلى الأصلين؛ فإنه مستفاد منهما، ومهما أقرَّ الخصم بالأصلين يلزمه - لا محالة - الإقرار بالفرع المستفاد منهما، وهو صحة الدعوى.
So when the pairing occurs according to its condition, it yields a third knowledge, which is the sought. And this third we may call a claim if we have an opponent, and we call it the sought as a name of the resulting knowledge if we have no opponent, because it is the seeker's object. And we call it a benefit and a branch in relation to the two roots, for it is derived from them. And whenever the opponent acknowledges the two roots, he is necessarily compelled to acknowledge the branch derived from them, which is the correctness of the claim.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines what might be called the first method of reasoning (al-manhaj al-awwal). It begins by describing the process of 'pairing' (izdiwaj) two premises (aslan) under a specific condition (shart) that ensures a necessary relationship. When this condition is met, the pairing produces a third piece of knowledge ('ilm thalith), which is the desired conclusion (al-matlab). Ghazali then explains that this third knowledge can be named differently depending on context: if there is an opponent, it is called a 'claim' (da'wa); if there is no opponent, it is simply called the 'sought' (matlab) because it is what the inquirer seeks. Additionally, relative to the two premises, it is called a 'benefit' (fa'ida) or a 'branch' (far'), since it is derived (mustafad) from them. The key logical point is that once the opponent acknowledges the two premises, he must necessarily acknowledge the derived branch—that is, the correctness of the claim. This establishes the foundational idea that a valid deduction forces assent from any rational interlocutor who accepts the premises.

Key Terms
الازدواج
الأصلان
المطلوب
الدعوى
الفائدة
الفرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any combination of two statements automatically produces a valid conclusion.
Correction: Ghazali emphasizes that the pairing must meet a specific condition ('ala shartihi) for the conclusion to follow necessarily. Not every combination yields a valid deduction.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the third knowledge called when there is no opponent?
A) الفائدة
B) المطلوب
C) الدعوى
D) الفرع

Key Takeaway
The first method of reasoning pairs two premises under a condition to yield a conclusion that must be accepted by anyone who acknowledges the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you acknowledge that Allah is the Creator and that the Qur'an is His final revelation, are you truly compelled to accept everything it commands? Or do you hold back, denying the branch while claiming to hold the roots?
?
Think of a belief you claim to hold that you have not yet acted upon. What are the two premises that should lead you to action? Do you honestly acknowledge both, or are you ignoring one to avoid the uncomfortable conclusion?
?
In your internal debates or discussions with others, do you follow the logical consequences of your own acknowledged premises, or do you stop short when the conclusion demands a change in your life?

The Second Method: A Concrete Syllogism
Ghazali presents a syllogistic proof for the world's origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses a concrete syllogism to demonstrate the coercive force of valid reasoning in establishing the world's origination.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism makes the conclusion inescapable.

Ghazali now provides a concrete example of the second method: a syllogism that proves the world is originated (hadith). By arranging two premises (roots) that any rational opponent must accept, he shows that the conclusion follows necessarily. This passage illustrates the logical structure that underpins kalām arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two premises
Find the two 'roots' (principles) Ghazali lists. Note how they are connected: the first is a general rule, the second applies it to the world.
The first premise defines everything that is not devoid of accidents as originated.
2
Identify the conclusion
What does Ghazali say follows from these two premises? That is the sought (matlab).
3
Consider the opponent's position
Ghazali asks you to imagine an opponent who accepts both premises but denies the conclusion. Why is this impossible?
Think about the logical necessity of a valid syllogism.
4
Reflect on the certainty produced
The passage ends by affirming that such denial is impossible. How does this method produce certain knowledge?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنهج الثاني : أن نرتب أصلين على وجه آخرَ : مثل قولنا : كلُّ ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث ، وهو أصل ، والعالم لا يخلو عن الحوادث ، وهو أصل آخرُ ؛ فيلزم منه صحةُ دعوانا وهو أن العالم حادث ، وهو المطلوب . فتأمل : هل يتصوَّرُ أن يقرَّ الخصمُ بالأصلين ثم يمكنه إنكار صحة إلزام الخصم بالنتيجة الدعوى ؟ فتعلم قطعاً أن ذلك محال .
The second method: that we arrange two roots in another manner, such as our saying: 'Everything that is not devoid of accidents is originated (hadith)'—this is a root; 'and the world is not devoid of accidents'—this is another root; then from it follows the correctness of our claim, which is that the world is originated, and that is the sought. So reflect: is it conceivable that the opponent acknowledges the two roots and then can deny the correctness of imposing upon the opponent the conclusion—the claim? Then you know with certainty that this is impossible.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the second method (al-manhaj al-thani) of reasoning. Ghazali gives a classic kalām argument for the world's temporal origination (ḥudūth). The first premise states that anything that is never free from accidents (ḥawādith) is itself originated (ḥādith). The second premise states that the world is not free from accidents. The conclusion necessarily follows: the world is originated. Ghazali then challenges the reader to consider whether an opponent who admits both premises can still deny the conclusion. The answer is a definitive 'no'—it is impossible (muḥāl). Thus, this method demonstrates the coercive force of a valid syllogism: once the premises are granted, the conclusion is inescapable.

Key Terms
المنهج الثاني
الأصل
الحوادث
حادث
العالم
المطلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An opponent could accept the premises but reject the conclusion due to some ambiguity in terms.
Correction: Ghazali insists that if the terms are clearly and unambiguously understood, the conclusion is logically binding. A valid syllogism leaves no room for denial once premises are granted.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the syllogism presented in the second method?
A) العالم حادث
B) كل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث
C) العالم لا يخلو عن الحوادث
D) صحة دعوى الخصم

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism with accepted premises yields an inescapable conclusion, proving the world's origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are faced with a clear logical argument that contradicts a belief you hold dear, do you submit to the truth or find ways to rationalize your resistance? What is one area of your faith where you have avoided such rigorous examination?
?
Ghazali says it is impossible to accept the two premises and then deny the conclusion. In your own life, when have you stubbornly rejected an obvious truth because it was uncomfortable? What did that cost you, and how can you be more open to truth in the future?
?
The syllogism forces certainty. Are you willing to let the truth of this argument—that the world is created and depends on a Creator—transform how you live today? What specific action will you take to align your life with this reality?
